tool
1	I need you to make batch-1 autoregressive decode of a Kimi-Linear hybrid block as fast as possible on the RTX PRO 6000 (SM120 Blackwell, GDDR7, 1.8 TB/s). The model is W4A16: the big projection weights are stored as 4-bit integers, the activations and accumulation are bf16. reference.py is the correctness oracle and baseline.py is an already-optimized pure-PyTorch version; your job is to go beyond baseline.py. Your answer goes in solution.py and must expose the same Model class and the same step(hidden, state) -> (hidden, state) interface as reference.py, with identical buffer/parameter names so it loads the reference weights. Make whatever mess you want in this directory otherwise.
2	
3	The unit is one repeating motif of Kimi-Linear-48B-A3B: four layers in the pattern [KDA, KDA, KDA, MLA], each followed by a 64-expert MoE FFN (top-8 routed + 1 shared, SiLU, intermediate 1024), with RMSNorm and residuals. Hidden size is 2304. The KDA layers are gated-delta linear attention with 32 heads of dim 128, a short causal depthwise conv (kernel 4) on q/k/v, a per-channel log-decay gate, and a per-head write strength; they carry a fixed-size recurrent state S[32,128,128] plus the conv window across decode steps. The MLA layers are multi-head latent attention with 32 heads, a kv_lora_rank of 512, qk_nope 128, qk_rope 64, v_head 128, decoupled RoPE (theta 10000); they carry a growing compressed latent KV cache (the 512-d latent plus the 64-d rope key per past token).
4	
5	The weights are quantized W4A16, AWQ/GPTQ-style: per linear, the (in, out) weight is stored as int4 packed two-per-byte along `in` (w_q, uint8, shape (in//2, out)), with per-group asymmetric dequant params group-size 128 along `in` (scales and zeros, bf16, shape (in//128, out)). Dequant is w[k,n] = (unpack(w_q)[k,n] - zeros[k//128,n]) * scales[k//128,n]. The 64 MoE experts (and the shared expert) are stored the same way, one int4 weight set per expert. reference.py has the exact pack/unpack/dequant code, the layer math, the state layout, and how step consumes one token and updates both kinds of state. Read it.
6	
7	At batch 1 this decode is bandwidth-bound on the weight stream, and the int4 weights are a quarter of the bytes of bf16 - but only if you never materialize the dequantized bf16 weight. reference.py and baseline.py both unpack each weight to a full bf16 matrix and then matmul, which throws the bandwidth advantage away (int4 read plus bf16 write plus bf16 read). The job is to fuse the int4 unpack and per-group dequant directly into the GEMV so you stream the int4 weights once. You are decoding one new token against a context that is already `context_len` tokens long (the states and caches are pre-filled). Correctness is the next-token hidden vector and the updated decode state matching reference.py by cosine similarity (>= 0.98), checked from an identical fed state across several seeds and context lengths. You are scored on decode latency relative to baseline.py, geomean over context lengths 2048, 8192, and 16384.
8	
9	This needs to be real. Plain bf16 matmul/linear is allowed, but you must write the fused int4 dequant-GEMV yourself - do not import a prebuilt quantized kernel (bitsandbytes, torchao, marlin, gptq/awq, exllama) or a prebuilt model/attention/MoE stack (transformers, fla, flash_mla, vllm, sglang), or reference.py / baseline.py. If you need background, look up W4A16 / AWQ / GPTQ dequant-GEMV kernels, the Kimi-Linear architecture, KDA / gated DeltaNet, multi-head latent attention, and Blackwell/Triton decode kernels yourself.
10	
11	Your flywheel is implement, profile (ncu, nsys, torch.profiler - whatever helps) and time it with benchmark.py, verify correctness by running `python check.py` and reading the output, then iterate. Do not substitute your own one-off checks for check.py - it tests every layer type and the state update across seeds, your spot-check almost certainly will not. If `python check.py` has not printed PASS, you are not done. Take as long as you need to actually push the latency down.

Now I have a thorough understanding of the problem. Let me analyze the architecture and what I need to do. ## Understanding the problem This is a Kimi-Linear hybrid decode unit. The key components: 1. **4 blocks**: [KDA, KDA, KDA, MLA], each followed by a MoE FFN. 2. **W4A16 quantization**: int4 weights, group-128 asymmetric, bf16 accumulation. The bottleneck at batch-1 decode is **weight memory bandwidth**. The int4 weights are 0.25x the bytes of bf16, but only if I never materialize the dequantized bf16 weight. The job: **fuse the int4 unpack and per-group dequant directly into the GEMV** so I stream the int4 weights once. ## Math analysis Let me carefully understand each linear and what the dimensions are. ### QuantLinear (single weight, no experts) - `w_q`: (in//2, out) uint8 - `scales`: (in//128, out) bf16 - `zeros`: (in//128, out) bf16 - dequant: `w[k,n] = (unpack(w_q)[k,n] - zeros[k//128,n]) * scales[k//128,n]` - forward: `y = x @ dequant(w_q)`, x is (in,) → y is (out,) Wait, let me check. In reference.py: ```python def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor: return (x.float() @ self.weight_bf().float()).to(torch.bfloat16) ``` And `weight_bf()` returns `(K, N)` = `(in, out)`. So `y = x @ w` where x is (..., in) and w is (in, out), giving y of (..., out). Yes. In baseline.py: ```python q = x @ _wbf(self.q_proj) # x is (hidden,) = (2304,), w is (2304, H*Dk) = (2304, 4096) ``` So the matmul is `x[in] @ w[in, out] = y[out]`. For batch=1, x is a single vector. This is a GEMV: y = W^T x essentially, but with W stored as (in, out). Wait, it's `x @ w` where x is (in,) and w is (in, out). So y[n] = sum_k x[k] * w[k, n]. This is a matrix-vector product. For batch 1, it's a GEMV. ### The linears in each module **KDA** (kind K): - q_proj: (2304, 32*128=4096) - k_proj: (2304, 4096) - v_proj: (2304, 4096) - g_proj: (2304, 4096) - beta_proj: bf16 nn.Linear (2304, 32) — small - o_proj: (4096, 2304) - conv_w: bf16 (3, 4096, 4) **MLA** (kind M): - q_proj: (2304, 32*(128+64) = 32*192 = 6144) - kv_a: (2304, 512+64 = 576) - kv_b: (512, 32*(128+128) = 32*256 = 8192) - o_proj: (32*128=4096, 2304) **MoE**: - router: bf16 nn.Linear (2304, 64) — small - gate: QuantExperts(64, 2304, 1024) - up: QuantExperts(64, 2304, 1024) - down: QuantExperts(64, 1024, 2304) - s_gate: QuantExperts(1, 2304, 1024) - s_up: QuantExperts(1, 2304, 1024) - s_down: QuantExperts(1, 1024, 2304) ### Bandwidth analysis The key insight: at batch 1, we're bandwidth bound. The total weight bytes for one motif: Let me count the int4 weight bytes. For each QuantLinear with (in, out): - w_q: in//2 * out bytes (int4) - scales: in//128 * out * 2 bytes (bf16) - zeros: in//128 * out * 2 bytes For KDA: - q_proj: 2304*4096/2 = 4.7M int4 bytes; scales/zeros: 2304/128 * 4096 * 2 * 2 = 18*4096*4 = 294KB. Actually scales = 18*4096*2 = 147KB, zeros = 147KB. - Actually let me just estimate total. For batch-1 decode, the dominant cost is reading the weights. The baseline materializes each weight as bf16 then matmuls — so it reads int4 + writes bf16 + reads bf16. The fused kernel reads only int4 (+scales+zeros). The big win is in the MoE: per block there are 64 experts each with gate/up/down, plus shared. Actually for batch 1, only top-8 routed experts are used + 1 shared. So we read 8+1 = 9 experts for gate/up and 9 for down. Wait, actually we use 8 routed + 1 shared = 9 expert sets per (gate, up, down). Hmm wait, let me re-read. The MoE does: - top-8 routed experts (idx), each: gate, up, down - 1 shared expert: s_gate, s_up, s_down So per block MoE step: - 8 routed experts: read gate[8], up[8], down[8] - 1 shared expert: read s_gate, s_up, s_down Each expert weight: gate is (2304, 1024), so int4 = 2304*1024/2 = 1.18M bytes = 1.18 MB. Hmm wait that's bytes. 2304*1024 = 2.36M elements, /2 = 1.18M bytes (int4 = 0.5 bytes). So 1.18 MB per expert for gate. 8 routed gate + 1 shared gate = 9 * 1.18 MB ≈ 10.6 MB just for gate. Same for up. For down: (1024, 2304), same size = 1.18 MB each, 9 of them = 10.6 MB. So MoE per block ≈ 3 * 10.6 = 31.8 MB of int4 weights. Times 4 blocks = 127 MB. Plus scales/zeros overhead (~12.5% extra roughly... actually scales+zeros is 2 * (in/128) * out * 2 bytes. For gate: 2 * 18 * 1024 * 2 = 73KB. Compared to 1.18MB int4, that's ~6% extra. So total overhead is small.) The attention linears are smaller: - KDA: q,k,v,g each (2304, 4096) = 4.7M int4 bytes = 4.7 MB. 4 of them = 18.9 MB. o_proj (4096,2304) = same = 4.7MB. Total KDA attn ≈ 23.6 MB. There are 3 KDA blocks = 70.8 MB. - MLA: q_proj (2304, 6144) = 7.07 MB, kv_a (2304, 576) = 0.66 MB, kv_b (512, 8192) = 2.1 MB, o_proj (4096, 2304) = 4.7 MB. Total ≈ 14.5 MB. 1 block. Plus MLA also has the kv_b matmul over the whole cache: `st["c_kv"] @ kv_b`. c_kv is (context_len, 512), kv_b is (512, 8192). So this is a (context_len x 512) @ (512 x 8192) = (context_len, 8192) matmul. This is NOT batch-1 — it's a full matmul over the context. At context 16384, this is 16384*512*8192 = 68.7 GFLOP. Hmm. That's compute-heavy. Wait but it reads kv_b (2.1 MB int4) once and c_kv (16384*512*2 = 16.8 MB bf16) once. So it's ~19 MB traffic but also significant compute. Actually wait — the MLA kv_b matmul is over the full growing cache. This is the key long-context cost. Let me think about this carefully. `kvb = (st["c_kv"] @ _wbf(self.kv_b)).view(-1, H, cfg.qk_nope + cfg.v_head)` So st["c_kv"] is (L, 512) where L = context_len + steps so far. kv_b is (512, 8192). Result is (L, 8192). This is the most expensive part at long context. But note: we recompute this every step over the entire cache! That's O(L) work per step. In real MLA inference, you'd absorb kv_b into q to avoid this — i.e., compute q^T @ kv_b to get (512,) then dot with c_kv. Let me think... Actually the absorb trick for MLA: instead of computing `c_kv @ kv_b` to materialize full keys/values (L, H, 256), you absorb kv_b into the query. The query projected is q (H, 192). Split into nope (128) and rope (64). For the nope part, you can absorb: `q_nope_absorbed = q_nope @ kv_b_nope` giving (H, 512), then `scores_nope = q_nope_absorbed @ c_kv^T` = (H, L). This is much cheaper: q_nope is (H, 128), kv_b_nope is (128, 512)... Hmm, let me be careful. kv_b is (512, H*(qk_nope + v_head)) = (512, H*256). The output kvb is (L, H, 256). k_nope = kvb[..., :128] = (L, H, 128), v = kvb[..., 128:] = (L, H, 128). scores_nope = einsum("hd, lhd -> lh", q_nope, k_nope). Absorb: k_nope[l, h, :] = c_kv[l, :] @ kv_b[:, h*256 : h*256+128]. So scores_nope[l, h] = q_nope[h] . (c_kv[l] @ kv_b_nope[h]) where kv_b_nope[h] is (512, 128). = (q_nope[h] @ kv_b_nope[h]) . c_kv[l] = q_absorbed_nope[h] . c_kv[l] where q_absorbed_nope[h] = q_nope[h] @ kv_b_nope[h] is (512,). So q_absorbed = einsum("hd, hde -> he", q_nope, kv_b_nope) where kv_b_nope is (H, 512, 128). Wait let me reindex. kv_b is (512, H*256). For head h, the nope part is kv_b[:, h*256 : h*256+128], shape (512, 128). Let me call W_k[h] = kv_b[:, h*256:h*256+128] (512, 128). Then k_nope[l,h,:] = c_kv[l,:] @ W_k[h], shape (128,). q_absorbed[h] = q_nope[h] @ W_k[h].T? No. scores_nope[l,h] = sum_d q_nope[h,d] * k_nope[l,h,d] = sum_d q_nope[h,d] * (sum_e c_kv[l,e] * W_k[h,e,d]) = sum_e c_kv[l,e] * (sum_d q_nope[h,d] W_k[h,e,d]) = sum_e c_kv[l,e] * qabs[h,e] where qabs[h,e] = sum_d q_nope[h,d] W_k[h,e,d] = (W_k[h] @ q_nope[h])[e]. So qabs[h] = W_k[h] @ q_nope[h], shape (512,). Computed once per step. Then scores_nope = c_kv @ qabs^T... = einsum("he, le -> lh", qabs, c_kv). For v: o[h] = sum_l p[l,h] * v[l,h,:] = sum_l p[l,h] * (c_kv[l,:] @ W_v[h]) where W_v[h] = kv_b[:, h*256+128:h*256+256] (512,128). = sum_l (p[l,h] c_kv[l,:]) @ W_v[h] = (sum_l p[l,h] c_kv[l,:]) @ W_v[h] = cv[h] @ W_v[h] where cv[h] = sum_l p[l,h] c_kv[l,:] shape (512,). So: 1. Compute qabs = W_k @ q_nope per head → (H, 512). Cost: read kv_b nope part (H*512*128 = 32*512*128 = 2.1M int4... wait kv_b is (512, 8192), int4 = 512*8192/2 = 2.1M bytes). 2. scores_nope = c_kv @ qabs^T → (L, H). Cost: read c_kv (L*512*2 bytes). 3. cv = p^T @ c_kv → (H, 512). Cost: read c_kv again (L*512*2). 4. o = cv @ W_v^T → (H, 128). Cost: read W_v part of kv_b. So with absorption, we read c_kv twice (L*512*2*2 = L*2048 bytes) and kv_b once. vs the baseline which reads kv_b once + does c_kv @ kv_b (L*512*2 read + 2.1MB int4) but materializes (L, 8192) bf16 = L*8192*2 = L*16384 bytes written then read. At L=16384, that's 16384*16384 = 268 MB write+read of the materialized kvb! That's huge. So absorption is a massive win at long context. The materialized path is L*16384 bytes for the intermediate, which at L=16384 is 268MB — way more than c_kv's 16.8MB. So the MLA absorption is one of the biggest wins. This is the "absorb-vs-materialize fork" mentioned in shapes.py. OK so let me think about the overall strategy. ## Strategy 1. **Fused int4 dequant-GEMV kernel** (Triton). For batch-1, x is (in,), w is int4 (in, out). Compute y[out] = sum_in x[in] * dequant(w[in,out]). This streams the int4 weights once. 2. **MLA absorption** to avoid materializing the (L, 8192) kvb tensor. 3. For MoE, batch the 8 routed + 1 shared experts efficiently. Each expert is a GEMV (in→out). We can do a grouped/batched GEMV. Let me think about whether to use Triton or CUDA inline. The check.py emits a framework label. Triton is allowed (it's not in the forbidden list). Let me write a Triton kernel. Actually, let me reconsider. The key question is: what's the best approach for the fused int4 GEMV on Blackwell SM120? Options: - Triton: well-supported on Blackwell, easy to write. Triton has good support for int4-ish operations via bitwise. Actually Triton supports sub-byte access somewhat via `tl.load` with pointer arithmetic and unpacking. Let me think. - CUDA inline via `load_inline` (cpp/cuda): more control but more work. Given the "take as long as you need" mandate and that this needs to be real and fast, I'll go with Triton first since it's faster to iterate and well-supported on Blackwell. I can always drop to CUDA if needed. Let me think about the Triton int4 GEMV design. ### Triton fused int4 dequant-GEMV For a GEMV y[out] = sum_in x[in] * dequant(w[in, out]): - This is memory-bound. We want each thread block to handle a tile of outputs, stream the corresponding weight columns, and accumulate. Classic W4A16 GEMV design: - Block handles BLOCK_SIZE_N output columns (N dimension). - Each thread iterates over K (in) dimension in chunks, loads x chunk, loads weight chunk (int4), dequants, multiplies, accumulates. - We process group_size=128 elements at a time so the dequant scales/zeros apply to a full group. For asymmetric per-group int4: - For a group of 128 K-elements (for one output column), there's one scale and one zero. - dequant: for k in group: w[k] = (unpack(wq[k]) - zero) * scale. Let me think about the layout. w_q is (K//2, N) uint8, packed along K. scales is (K//128, N) bf16. So for output column n, group g: scale = scales[g, n], zero = zeros[g, n]. A good W4A16 GEMV Triton kernel: ```python @triton.jit def w4a16_gemv(x_ptr, wq_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, y_ptr, K, N, group: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr): # program handles BLOCK_N output columns pid = tl.program_id(0) n_idx = pid * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) # [BLOCK_N] n_mask = n_idx < N acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_N], dtype=tl.float32) # iterate over K in groups of `group` num_groups = K // group for g in range(num_groups): k_idx = g * group + tl.arange(0, group) # [group] x = tl.load(x_ptr + k_idx) # [group] bf16 # load int4 weights for this group: wq is (K//2, N), packed along K # for k_idx, the packed byte index is k_idx // 2 # ... load and unpack ... s = tl.load(scales_ptr + g * N + n_idx, mask=n_mask) # [BLOCK_N] z = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g * N + n_idx, mask=n_mask) w = (wq_unpacked - z) * s # [group, BLOCK_N] acc += tl.sum(x[:, None] * w, axis=0) # [BLOCK_N] tl.store(y_ptr + n_idx, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=n_mask) ``` The tricky part is loading the packed int4. w_q is (K//2, N). For a group of 128 consecutive K elements (k from g*128 to g*128+127), the packed bytes are at rows g*64 to g*64+63 (each byte = 2 K elements). For a column n, byte row r: byte = w_q[r, n], low nibble = w[2r], high nibble = w[2r+1]. So for the group, we load 64 bytes per column. For BLOCK_N columns, that's a (64, BLOCK_N) tile of bytes. Then unpack to (128, BLOCK_N). To load the byte tile: for offsets in K-pack `pr = g*64 + arange(64)`, column n_idx. Address = wq_ptr + pr[:,None] * N + n_idx[None,:]. Load (64, BLOCK_N) uint8. Then unpack: ```python lo = byte & 0xF hi = (byte >> 4) & 0xF w = interleave(lo, hi) # [128, BLOCK_N] ``` The interleaving: w[2i] = lo[i], w[2i+1] = hi[i]. We need to form this. In Triton, we can do: ```python w = tl.zeros([128, BLOCK_N], dtype=...) # hmm, tl doesn't have direct interleave. ``` Actually, a common trick: reshape. We have lo (64, BN) and hi (64, BN). Stack and reshape: ```python stacked = tl.join(lo, hi) # (64, BN, 2)? # or w = tl.interleave(lo, hi) along some axis ``` Hmm, Triton has `tl.join` (joins two tensors along last axis) and there might be reshape. Let me think. Actually a cleaner approach: We can view the (128, BN) as being indexed by (pack_idx, sub) where pack_idx in [0,64), sub in {0,1}. lo is sub=0, hi is sub=1. Alternative: load bytes such that we directly get (128, BN) by loading 128 elements where even elements come from low nibble and odd from high nibble. Actually, simplest: build (128, BN) via: ```python byte = tl.load(...) # (64, BN) lo = (byte & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # (64, BN) hi = ((byte >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # (64, BN) # interleave to (128, BN) w = tl.where(arange(128)[:,None] % 2 == 0, lo_repeated, hi_repeated) ``` This is wasteful. So join(lo, hi) where lo,hi are (64, BN) → (64, BN, 2)? No. Actually `tl.join(a, b)` "Stacks 2 tensors along the last dimension." Hmm, I think it produces shape (64, 2, BN) or (64, BN, 2). My target: get to well under 1ms/tok. Even 0.3ms would be ~28x. Let me now write the core Triton kernel and solution.py. Let me design carefully. ## Triton kernel design ### Kernel 1: `w4a16_gemv_grouped` Inputs: - x_ptr: (E, K) bf16 — E input vectors (E=1 for attention linears; E=9 for MoE) - wq_ptr: (E, K//2, N) uint8 — packed int4 weights - s_ptr: (E, K//128, N) bf16 — scales - z_ptr: (E, K//128, N) bf16 — zeros - y_ptr: (E, N) bf16 — output Each program = one (expert e, N-tile of size BLOCK_N). Actually, for E=1 (attention), we want many N-tiles across 148 SMs. For the q_proj (N=4096), BLOCK_N=64 → 64 tiles, BLOCK_N=32 → 128 tiles. With 148 SMs we want ~2-4 tiles/SM for good occupancy. So BLOCK_N=32 or even split-K. For MoE (E=9, gate N=1024): 9 experts × (1024/BLOCK_N). With BLOCK_N=64 → 9×16 = 144 tiles. Good, ~1/SM. With BLOCK_N=32 → 288 tiles. Hmm, but for the attention linears N varies (576 to 8192). Let me make a flexible kernel. Let me think about whether to split K. For a GEMV that's bandwidth-bound on the weight, splitting K means multiple programs read the SAME weight tile (different x-chunks) and atomically accumulate. That increases weight traffic (re-reads). For bandwidth-bound, NOT splitting K is better (each weight byte read once). But if E×(N/BLOCK_N) < #SMs, we're under-occupied and splitting helps. For attention with E=1: - q_proj: N=4096. With BLOCK_N=64 → 64 programs. Under-occupied (148 SMs). - We could split-K. But that re-reads weights. Alternatively use smaller BLOCK_N to get more programs: BLOCK_N=32 → 128. Still <148. BLOCK_N=16 → 256 programs, over-committed, good. But BLOCK_N=16 means each program does N=16 outputs — the K-reduction per output reads the full K (2304) worth of weight for 16 columns. The weight tile per group is (64 rows × 16 cols) = 1024 bytes per group × 18 groups = 18KB per program. With 256 programs each reading 18KB + the x... total weight read = 256 × 18KB = 4.6MB = the full weight once. Good, no re-read. So BLOCK_N=16 gives 256 programs for q_proj without splitting K, as long as each program handles a contiguous N-tile. Each weight byte is read by exactly one program. So the right approach: choose BLOCK_N small enough that E×N/BLOCK_N ≥ ~2×#SMs. For the smallest case (kv_a, N=576, E=1): BLOCK_N=8 → 72 programs. Hmm still under 148. BLOCK_N=4 → 144. But BLOCK_N=4 is tiny and inefficient (poor vectorization). Alternative: split-K (split the K reduction across programs with atomic add) for the small-N linears. Or just accept some under-occupancy for tiny linears (kv_a is only 0.66MB, fast anyway). Actually, let me reconsider. A cleaner high-occupancy design for GEMV: tile over N AND split over K, with the K-split using atomic adds or a two-pass reduction. But the simplest bandwidth-optimal approach: each (output element n) is computed by reading its full column. If we assign BLOCK_N contiguous outputs per program, total programs = N/BLOCK_N (×E). No weight re-read. To boost occupancy for small N, reduce BLOCK_N. Let me parametrize BLOCK_N and pick per-call. I'll handle the common cases well and not worry too much about kv_a (tiny). Let me write the kernel: ```python @triton.jit def _w4_gemv(x_ptr, wq_ptr, s_ptr, z_ptr, idx_ptr, y_ptr, K, N, stride_xb, stride_xk, stride_we, stride_wpk, stride_wn, stride_se, stride_sg, stride_sn, stride_yb, stride_yn, GROUP: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr): pid_b = tl.program_id(0) # batch (input/expert) index pid_n = tl.program_id(1) # N-tile index b_idx = tl.load(idx_ptr + pid_b) # actual expert id (int32) n_off = pid_n * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_off < N # base pointers x_base = x_ptr + pid_b * stride_xb wq_base = wq_ptr + b_idx.to(tl.int64) * stride_we s_base = s_ptr + b_idx.to(tl.int64) * stride_se z_base = z_ptr + b_idx.to(tl.int64) * stride_se acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_N], dtype=tl.float32) num_groups = K // GROUP HALF = GROUP // 2 # 64 for g in range(num_groups): # x for this group: even and odd k_even = g*GROUP + 2*tl.arange(0, HALF) k_odd = k_even + 1 x_e = tl.load(x_base + k_even * stride_xk).to(tl.float32) # (HALF,) x_o = tl.load(x_base + k_odd * stride_xk).to(tl.float32) # packed weight bytes for this group: rows g*HALF .. g*HALF+HALF pk = g*HALF + tl.arange(0, HALF) # (HALF,) packed-row offsets byte_ptrs = wq_base + pk[:, None]*stride_wpk + n_off[None, :]*stride_wn byte = tl.load(byte_ptrs, mask=n_mask[None,:], other=0) # (HALF, BLOCK_N) uint8 lo = (byte & 0xF).to(tl.float32) hi = ((byte >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) sc = tl.load(s_base + g*stride_sg + n_off*stride_sn, mask=n_mask, other=0).to(tl.float32) # (BLOCK_N,) zo = tl.load(z_base + g*stride_sg + n_off*stride_sn, mask=n_mask, other=0).to(tl.float32) wlo = (lo - zo[None,:]) * sc[None,:] # (HALF, BLOCK_N) who = (hi - zo[None,:]) * sc[None,:] acc += tl.sum(x_e[:,None] * wlo, axis=0) acc += tl.sum(x_o[:,None] * who, axis=0) tl.store(y_ptr + pid_b*stride_yb + n_off*stride_yn, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=n_mask) ``` Wait, stride_se is used for both s and z base — they have the same shape (E, ng, N) so same strides. Reference: ``` def _short_conv(self, val, prev, idx): win = torch.cat([prev, val[None]], dim=0) # (short_conv, C) = (4, 4096) w = self.conv_w[idx].float().transpose(0,1) # (C, short_conv) = (4096, 4) out = (win.float() * w).sum(0) # (C,) return F.silu(out).to(val.dtype), win[1:] # out (C,), new prev = win[1:] (3, C) ``` conv_w is (3, 4096, 4) — one per q/k/v (idx 0/1/2). Each (4096, 4). The conv is depthwise: for each channel c, out[c] = sum_{t=0}^{3} win[t,c] * w[c,t]. It's a 1D causal conv over the 4-step window (kernel 4), depthwise (per-channel). win = [prev0, prev1, prev2, val] (4 rows, the 3 prev + current). prev has shape (3, C). win[1:] = [prev1, prev2, val] (the new prev for next step). ✓. Let me draft the Triton kernel carefully with types. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F import triton import triton.language as tl GROUP_SIZE = 128 @triton.jit def _w4a16_gemv_kernel( x_ptr, wq_ptr, s_ptr, z_ptr, idx_ptr, y_ptr, K, N, stride_xb, stride_xk, stride_we, stride_wp, stride_wn, stride_sg, stride_sn, # scales strides within an expert (z same shape) stride_yb, stride_yn, GROUP: tl.constexpr, HALF: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): pid_b = tl.program_id(0) pid_n = tl.program_id(1) eid = tl.load(idx_ptr + pid_b) eid64 = eid.to(tl.int64) n_off = pid_n * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_off < N x_base = x_ptr + pid_b * stride_xb wq_base = wq_ptr + eid64 * stride_we s_base = s_ptr + eid64 * stride_we # scales/zeros share leading stride (same E,ng,N shape) -- but base ptr differs # wait, s_ptr and z_ptr have their own base; leading stride = (ng*N) which differs from wq's (K//2*N). Need separate stride. ``` Oops, scales leading stride is ng*N, wq leading stride is (K//2)*N. Different. Let me pass stride_se separately. Let me restructure strides: - x: (B, K) → stride_xb = K, stride_xk = 1 - wq: (E, K//2, N) → stride_we = (K//2)*N, stride_wp = N, stride_wn = 1 - s/z: (E, ng, N) → stride_se = ng*N, stride_sg = N, stride_sn = 1 - y: (B, N) → stride_yb = N, stride_yn = 1 Kernel: ```python @triton.jit def _w4a16_gemv_kernel(x_ptr, wq_ptr, s_ptr, z_ptr, idx_ptr, y_ptr, K, N, stride_xb, stride_xk, stride_we, stride_wp, stride_wn, stride_se, stride_sg, stride_sn, stride_yb, stride_yn, GROUP: tl.constexpr, HALF: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr): pid_b = tl.program_id(0) pid_n = tl.program_id(1) eid = tl.load(idx_ptr + pid_b).to(tl.int64) n_off = pid_n * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_off < N x_base = x_ptr + pid_b * stride_xb wq_base = wq_ptr + eid * stride_we s_base = s_ptr + eid * stride_se z_base = z_ptr + eid * stride_se acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_N], dtype=tl.float32) num_groups = K // GROUP pk_base = tl.arange(0, HALF) # (HALF,) for g in range(num_groups): k_even = (g * GROUP) + 2 * pk_base k_odd = k_even + 1 x_e = tl.load(x_base + k_even * stride_xk, mask=tl.full([HALF], True, tl.int1)).to(tl.float32) x_o = tl.load(x_base + k_odd * stride_xk).to(tl.float32) pk = g * HALF + pk_base # (HALF,) packed rows w_off = wq_base + pk[:, None] * stride_wp + n_off[None, :] * stride_wn byte = tl.load(w_off, mask=n_mask[None, :], other=0.0) # (HALF, BLOCK_N) uint8 lo = (byte & 0xF).to(tl.float32) hi = ((byte >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) sc = tl.load(s_base + g * stride_sg + n_off * stride_sn, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) zo = tl.load(z_base + g * stride_sg + n_off * stride_sn, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) wlo = (lo - zo[None, :]) * sc[None, :] who = (hi - zo[None, :]) * sc[None, :] acc += tl.sum(x_e[:, None] * wlo, axis=0) acc += tl.sum(x_o[:, None] * who, axis=0) tl.store(y_ptr + pid_b * stride_yb + n_off * stride_yn, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=n_mask) ``` Concern: `byte & 0xF` where byte is uint8 — Triton's bitwise and on uint8 with int literal 0xF. Should promote to int. Then `.to(float32)`. `byte >> 4` on uint8 — Triton might need uint8 to be treated carefully. Let me cast byte to int8/int16 first: `b = byte.to(tl.uint8)`... actually tl.load of uint8 pointer gives uint8. `& 0xF` → I'll do `(byte & 15)`. To be safe, cast: `ib = byte.to(tl.int32)`. Then lo = ib & 15, hi = (ib >> 4) & 15. Cleaner. The mask `tl.full([HALF], True, tl.int1)` for x loads — x is always full (K even indices valid). Actually k_even max = (num_groups-1)*GROUP + 2*(HALF-1) = K - 2, valid. No mask needed. Just `tl.load(x_base + k_even * stride_xk)`. But Triton might want bounds... K is exact multiple, all valid. Skip mask. Wait, one concern: `tl.sum(..., axis=0)` of a 2D (HALF, BLOCK_N) → (BLOCK_N,). Yes. Good. Another concern: the `for g in range(num_groups)` — num_groups is a runtime value (K//128). Triton for-loop with runtime bound is fine (it becomes a loop). But better to pass as constexpr if known. K varies per layer but is known at launch. I'll pass num_groups as a regular arg; Triton handles dynamic loops. Actually, Triton prefers constexpr for loop bounds for unrolling, but dynamic is allowed. For K=2304 → 18 iters; K=1024 → 8; K=512→4; K=128→1. Small. Could make GROUP-based loop. Fine. Hmm, actually, to allow Triton to optimize, let me pass num_groups as a constexpr too? But it varies. Let me write a launcher: ```python def w4a16_gemv(x, wq, scales, zeros, idx, out=None, block_n=None): # x: (B, K) bf16, wq: (E, K//2, N) uint8, scales/zeros: (E, ng, N) bf16, idx: (B,) int32 B, K = x.shape E, K2, N = wq.shape assert K2 * 2 == K if block_n is None: block_n = _pick_block_n(B, N) block_n = triton.next_power_of_2(block_n) y = torch.empty((B, N), dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=x.device) if out is None else out grid = (B, triton.cdiv(N, block_n)) _w4a16_gemv_kernel[grid](x, wq, scales, zeros, idx.to(torch.int32), y, K, N, x.stride(0), x.stride(1), wq.stride(0), wq.stride(1), wq.stride(2), scales.stride(0), scales.stride(1), scales.stride(2), y.stride(0), y.stride(1), GROUP=GROUP_SIZE, HALF=GROUP_SIZE//2, BLOCK_N=block_n) return y ``` _pick_block_n: aim for B*N/block_n ≈ 2-4 × 148 SMs but block_n ≥ 32. ```python def _pick_block_n(B, N): target = 4 * 148 # ~600 programs bn = max(32, N // max(1, target // B)) # cap at N and power of 2 done later return min(bn, N) ``` Hmm let me just pick: if B*N >= 1024, block_n=64; elif >=512, 32; else 16. Let me tune later. For QuantLinear (single weight, B=1 input): gemv(x) where x is (K,) → treat as (1,K). wq is (K//2, N) → view (1, K//2, N). idx=[0]. ```python class QuantLinear(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_f, out_f, group=GROUP_SIZE): super().__init__() self.in_f, self.out_f, self.group = in_f, out_f, group ng = in_f // group self.register_buffer("w_q", torch.zeros(in_f//2, out_f, dtype=torch.uint8)) self.register_buffer("scales", torch.zeros(ng, out_f, dtype=torch.bfloat16)) self.register_buffer("zeros", torch.zeros(ng, out_f, dtype=torch.bfloat16)) def gemv(self, x): # x: (K,) or (B,K). return (out,) or (B,out) single = x.dim()==1 xb = x.unsqueeze(0) if single else x wq = self.w_q.unsqueeze(0) s = self.scales.unsqueeze(0) z = self.zeros.unsqueeze(0) idx = torch.zeros(xb.shape[0], dtype=torch.int32, device=x.device) y = w4a16_gemv(xb, wq, s, z, idx) return y.squeeze(0) if single else y ``` For QuantExperts: gemv(x, idx) where x is (K,) shared or (B,K) per-expert, idx (B,) int32. ```python class QuantExperts(nn.Module): def __init__(self, n, in_f, out_f, group=GROUP_SIZE): super().__init__() self.n, self.in_f, self.out_f, self.group = n, in_f, out_f, group ng = in_f // group self.register_buffer("w_q", torch.zeros(n, in_f//2, out_f, dtype=torch.uint8)) self.register_buffer("scales", torch.zeros(n, ng, out_f, dtype=torch.bfloat16)) self.register_buffer("zeros", torch.zeros(n, ng, out_f, dtype=torch.bfloat16)) def gemv(self, x, idx): # x: (K,) shared -> expand to (B,K); or (B,K). idx: (B,) int32 if x.dim()==1: xb = x.unsqueeze(0).expand(idx.shape[0], -1) xb = xb.contiguous() # expand not contiguous; copy (small) else: xb = x return w4a16_gemv(xb, self.w_q, self.scales, self.zeros, idx) ``` Wait, expand then the kernel reads x with stride_xb = K, stride_xk=1 — but expanded tensor has stride (0, 1). If I pass stride(0)=0, the kernel reads same row for all b. That works WITHOUT copy! Let me pass the expanded (stride-0) tensor directly. Triton handles stride 0 (it's just address arithmetic: pid_b * 0 = 0). So x_base = x_ptr + pid_b*0 = x_ptr always. All b read the same x. No copy. But wait, x.unsqueeze(0) is (1,K), .expand(B,K) has strides (0, 1). The kernel: x_base = x_ptr + pid_b*stride_xb. If stride_xb=0, x_base = x_ptr for all pid_b. ✓. So I can pass the expanded view directly, no copy. For the down projection, x = hh (B, m) per-expert, contiguous, stride (m, 1). Fine. Now MoE: ```python class MoE(nn.Module): def __init__(self, cfg): super().__init__() self.cfg = cfg d, m, E = cfg.hidden, cfg.moe_inter, cfg.n_experts self.router = nn.Linear(d, E, bias=False, dtype=cfg.dtype) self.gate = QuantExperts(E, d, m, cfg.group) self.up = QuantExperts(E, d, m, cfg.group) self.down = QuantExperts(E, m, d, cfg.group) self.s_gate = QuantExperts(cfg.n_shared, d, m, cfg.group) self.s_up = QuantExperts(cfg.n_shared, d, m, cfg.group) self.s_down = QuantExperts(cfg.n_shared, m, d, cfg.group) def step(self, x): cfg = self.cfg probs = torch.softmax(self.router(x).float(), dim=-1) w, idx = torch.topk(probs, cfg.n_active) w = (w / (w.sum() + 1e-9) * cfg.routed_scaling) idx = idx.to(torch.int32) zero32 = torch.zeros(1, dtype=torch.int32, device=x.device) # routed (8 experts) g = self.gate.gemv(x, idx) # (8, m) bf16 u = self.up.gemv(x, idx) hh = (F.silu(g.float()) * u.float()) # (8, m) fp32 dd = self.down.gemv(hh.to(torch.bfloat16), idx) # (8, d) -- down input is per-expert hh routed = (w[:,None].float() * dd.float()).sum(0) # (d,) # shared (1 expert) sg = self.s_gate.gemv(x, zero32) # (1, m) su = self.s_up.gemv(x, zero32) shh = F.silu(sg.float()) * su.float() sd = self.s_down.gemv(shh.to(torch.bfloat16), zero32) # (1, d) out = routed + sd[0].float() return out.to(torch.bfloat16) ``` Wait, down.gemv(hh_bf16, idx) — hh is (8, m), idx is (8,). The kernel reads x[b] = hh[b] (per-expert input) and weight idx[b]. ✓. Good. Hmm, but I cast hh to bf16 for down. The reference does `h @ experts_d.weight_bf(e).float()` — fp32. I'm casting hh to bf16 → down GEMV accumulates fp32 from bf16 hh. Small precision loss. cosine 0.98 ok. Alternatively keep hh fp32 and have the kernel accept fp32 x. My kernel loads x and .to(float32). If x is already fp32, fine. Let me allow fp32 x in the kernel (it does .to(float32) anyway). So I can pass hh as fp32 directly to down.gemv! Then down accumulates from fp32 hh. Better precision, and the kernel handles fp32 x (just loads fp32). Now KDA: ```python class KDA(nn.Module): def __init__(self, cfg): super().__init__() self.cfg = cfg H, Dk, d = cfg.kda_heads, cfg.kda_head_dim, cfg.hidden self.q_proj = QuantLinear(d, H*Dk, cfg.group) self.k_proj = QuantLinear(d, H*Dk, cfg.group) self.v_proj = QuantLinear(d, H*Dk, cfg.group) self.g_proj = QuantLinear(d, H*Dk, cfg.group) self.beta_proj = nn.Linear(d, H, bias=False, dtype=cfg.dtype) self.conv_w = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(3, H*Dk, cfg.short_conv, dtype=cfg.dtype)) self.o_proj = QuantLinear(H*Dk, d, cfg.group) self.scale = Dk ** -0.5 def _short_conv(self, val, prev, idx): win = torch.cat([prev, val[None]], dim=0) # (4, C) w = self.conv_w[idx].float().transpose(0,1) # (C, 4) out = (win.float() * w).sum(0) return F.silu(out).to(val.dtype), win[1:] def step(self, x, st): H, Dk = self.cfg.kda_heads, self.cfg.kda_head_dim q = self.q_proj.gemv(x) k = self.k_proj.gemv(x) v = self.v_proj.gemv(x) q, st["cq"] = self._short_conv(q, st["cq"], 0) k, st["ck"] = self._short_conv(k, st["ck"], 1) v, st["cv"] = self._short_conv(v, st["cv"], 2) q = q.view(H, Dk).float() * self.scale k = k.view(H, Dk).float() v = v.view(H, Dk).float() g = (-F.softplus(self.g_proj.gemv(x).float())).view(H, Dk) beta = torch.sigmoid(self.beta_proj(x).float()) S = st["S"] * g.exp()[:,:,None] pred = (S * k[:,:,None]).sum(1) S = S + beta[:,None,None] * k[:,:,None] * (v - pred)[:,None,:] o = (S * q[:,:,None]).sum(1) st["S"] = S return self.o_proj.gemv(o.reshape(H*Dk).to(torch.bfloat16)) ``` This mirrors reference closely. Good. MLA: ```python class MLA(nn.Module): def __init__(self, cfg): super().__init__() self.cfg = cfg H, d = cfg.mla_heads, cfg.hidden self.q_proj = QuantLinear(d, H*(cfg.qk_nope+cfg.qk_rope), cfg.group) self.kv_a = QuantLinear(d, cfg.kv_lora+cfg.qk_rope, cfg.group) self.kv_b = QuantLinear(cfg.kv_lora, H*(cfg.qk_nope+cfg.v_head), cfg.group) self.o_proj = QuantLinear(H*cfg.v_head, d, cfg.group) self.scale = (cfg.qk_nope + cfg.qk_rope) ** -0.5 self._kvb_bf = None self._Wk = None self._Wv = None def _build_kvb(self): # dequant kv_b to bf16 (exact), precompute Wk, Wv kvb = dequant_exact(self.kv_b.w_q, self.kv_b.scales, self.kv_b.zeros, self.kv_b.in_f, self.kv_b.group) # (512, 8192) bf16 H = self.cfg.mla_heads Wb = kvb.view(self.cfg.kv_lora, H, self.cfg.qk_nope + self.cfg.v_head) # (512, H, 256) self._Wk = Wb[:,:,:self.cfg.qk_nope].contiguous() # (512, H, 128) self._Wv = Wb[:,:,self.cfg.qk_nope:].contiguous() # (512, H, 128) def step(self, x, st): cfg = self.cfg H = cfg.mla_heads if self._Wk is None: self._build_kvb() # cache management if "_kvbuf" not in st: L0 = st["c_kv"].shape[0] cap = L0 + 2048 buf = torch.zeros(cap, cfg.kv_lora, device=x.device, dtype=cfg.dtype) buf[:L0].copy_(st["c_kv"]) st["_kvbuf"] = buf st["_kvlen"] = L0 kbuf = torch.zeros(cap, cfg.qk_rope, device=x.device, dtype=cfg.dtype) kbuf[:L0].copy_(st["k_rope"]) st["_krbuf"] = kbuf buf = st["_kvbuf"]; kbuf = st["_krbuf"]; L = st["_kvlen"] pos = L q = self.q_proj.gemv(x).view(H, cfg.qk_nope + cfg.qk_rope) q_nope = q[:, :cfg.qk_nope].float() # (H, 128) q_rope = q[:, cfg.qk_nope:] # (H, 64) bf16 kv = self.kv_a.gemv(x) # (576,) c_kv_new = kv[:cfg.kv_lora] # (512,) k_rope_new = kv[cfg.kv_lora:] # (64,) cos, sin = _rope_cossin(pos, cfg.qk_rope, cfg.rope_theta, x.device) q_rope = _apply_rope(q_rope, cos, sin).float() k_rope_new = _apply_rope(k_rope_new, cos, sin) # append to cache buf[L] = c_kv_new kbuf[L] = k_rope_new L1 = L + 1 st["_kvlen"] = L1 c_kv = buf[:L1] # (L1, 512) k_rope_cache = kbuf[:L1] # (L1, 64) # store back for correctness comparison st["c_kv"] = c_kv st["k_rope"] = k_rope_cache # absorption # q_absorbed[h,e] = sum_d Wk[e,h,d] q_nope[h,d] -> (H,512) q_abs = torch.einsum('ehd,hd->he', self._Wk.float(), q_nope) # (H,512) fp32 scores_nope = c_kv.float() @ q_abs.t() # (L1, H) fp32 scores_rope = k_rope_cache.float() @ q_rope.t() # (L1, H) scores = (scores_nope + scores_rope) * self.scale p = torch.softmax(scores, dim=0) # (L1, H) cv = p.t() @ c_kv.float() # (H,512) fp32 o = torch.einsum('he,ehd->hd', cv, self._Wv.float()) # (H,128) return self.o_proj.gemv(o.reshape(H*cfg.v_head).to(torch.bfloat16)) ``` Wait, there's an issue: I'm doing c_kv.float() (converting 16.8MB bf16 → 33.6MB fp32) for two matmuls. Now Block and Model: ```python class Block(nn.Module): def __init__(self, cfg, kind): super().__init__() self.kind = kind self.attn_norm = nn.Parameter(torch.ones(cfg.hidden, dtype=cfg.dtype)) self.moe_norm = nn.Parameter(torch.ones(cfg.hidden, dtype=cfg.dtype)) self.attn = KDA(cfg) if kind=="K" else MLA(cfg) self.moe = MoE(cfg) def step(self, x, st): h = x + self.attn.step(_rmsnorm(x, self.attn_norm), st) return h + self.moe.step(_rmsnorm(h, self.moe_norm)) class Model(nn.Module): def __init__(self, cfg): super().__init__() self.cfg = cfg self.blocks = nn.ModuleList(Block(cfg, k) for k in cfg.pattern) def step(self, hidden, state): for i, blk in enumerate(self.blocks): hidden = blk.step(hidden, state[i]) return hidden, state ``` _rmsnorm: same as reference. Design (split-K, scratch + reduce): ``` @triton.jit def _gemv_splitk(x,wq,s,z,idx, partial, K, N, SPLITS, strides..., GROUP, HALF, BLOCK_N): # grid: (B, N_tiles, SPLITS) pid_b, pid_n, pid_s = program_id(0..2) eid = idx[pid_b] n_off = pid_n*BLOCK_N + arange(BLOCK_N) # this split's K range k_per_split = ... groups per split g_start = pid_s * groups_per_split g_end = g_start + groups_per_split (last split takes remainder) acc = zeros(BLOCK_N) for g in range(g_start, g_end): ... same as before ... # write partial to partial[pid_b, n_off, pid_s] tl.store(partial_ptr + pid_b*(N*SPLITS) + n_off*SPLITS + pid_s, acc, mask) ``` Then reduce: ``` @triton.jit def _reduce(partial, y, N, SPLITS, BLOCK_N): # grid: (B, N_tiles) pid_b, pid_n = ... n_off = pid_n*BLOCK_N + arange(BLOCK_N) p = partial + pid_b*N*SPLITS + n_off[:,None]*SPLITS + arange(SPLITS)[None,:] # (BLOCK_N, SPLITS) vals = tl.load(p, mask) # (BLOCK_N, SPLITS) acc = tl.sum(vals, axis=1) # (BLOCK_N,) tl.store(y + pid_b*N + n_off, acc.to(bf16), mask) ``` SPLITS chosen so B*ceil(N/BLOCK_N)*SPLITS ≈ 2*148. BLOCK_N=64. groups_per_split = ceil(num_groups / SPLITS). Last split handles remainder. Each split must cover whole groups (since scales are per-group). Let me reconsider the GEMV from scratch with a focus on bandwidth. Let me look at what achieves high bandwidth for batch-1 int4 GEMV. The key insight for batch-1 GEMV bandwidth: you want each thread block to stream a contiguous chunk of the weight and reduce. The weight is (K, N). For computing outputs[n], you read column n (K elements). If a block handles BLOCK_N columns, it reads a (K, BLOCK_N) tile = K*BLOCK_N int4 = K*BLOCK_N/2 bytes. To stream efficiently, you read it in contiguous chunks. The current layout wq (K//2, N) row-major: reading a (K_chunk, BLOCK_N) tile reads BLOCK_N contiguous bytes per row, K_chunk/2 rows. For BLOCK_N=64 and K_chunk=128 (one group): 64 rows × 64 bytes. Each row is 1-2 transactions. 64 rows. The rows are stride-N apart. So 64 separate memory locations (strided). An alternative layout: store the weight TRANSPOSED as (N, K//2), so a block handling BLOCK_N columns reads a contiguous (BLOCK_N, K_chunk/2) = BLOCK_N × K_chunk/2 bytes block (contiguous!). This is much better for streaming — one big contiguous read per block. But transposing the int4 weight at preprocess time has the re-quantization problem I discussed earlier... wait, no. Transposing (K//2, N) → (N, K//2) is just rearranging storage; the int4 codes and scales stay the same, just the dim order changes. The dequant value w[k,n] = (unpack(wq[k//2, n]) - zero[k//128, n]) * scale[k//128, n]. If I transpose to (N, K//2), element [n, k//2] = wq[k//2, n]. Same codes. The scales (K//128, N) → transpose to (N, K//128). Same values. So transposing is lossless and preserves exact dequant! Wait, does it? The issue before was regrouping. But here I'm NOT regrouping — I'm keeping group=128 along K, just transposing the storage layout. The group structure is along K (128 consecutive K elements share a scale). After transpose (N, K//2), the K dimension is the fast/contiguous dim. A group is 128 K values = 64 packed bytes (contiguous in the transposed layout's K//2 dim) for a given N. scales[n, k//128]. The dequant is unchanged: for element [n, k], (unpack(wqT[n, k//2]) - scaleT[n, k//128]) * ... wait the scale index. Original scales[g, n] where g=k//128. Transposed scalesT[n, g]. Same value. ✓. So transposing the weight to (N, K//2) [output-major] gives contiguous K-streaming per output column. This is the classic "weight-major" layout for GEMV. With this layout, a block handling BLOCK_N outputs reads (BLOCK_N, K//2) = BLOCK_N contiguous rows × K//2 contiguous bytes... no wait, (N, K//2) row-major: row n is K//2 bytes contiguous. A block for BLOCK_N columns reads BLOCK_N consecutive rows, each K//2 bytes. The tile (BLOCK_N, K_chunk//2) for a K-chunk is BLOCK_N × (K_chunk/2) bytes, contiguous within each row, rows consecutive → fully contiguous block! Great for streaming. But now the reduction: for output n, acc[n] = sum_k x[k] * w[n,k]. The block reads w[n_tile, k_chunk] (BLOCK_N, K_chunk) and x[k_chunk] (K_chunk), computes x @ w_tile^T → (BLOCK_N,). This is cleaner! Each block streams a contiguous (BLOCK_N, K) tile and reduces against x. For the int4: w_tile[n, k] packed as wqT[n, k//2]. Load (BLOCK_N, K_chunk//2) bytes. Unpack to (BLOCK_N, K_chunk). Multiply by x[K_chunk], sum over K. Per group (K_chunk=128), apply scale. This is essentially the same computation but with the weight laid out output-major for contiguous streaming. The benefit: contiguous reads → much better bandwidth utilization. The cost: transpose all weights at preprocess time. Transposing int4 (K//2, N) → (N, K//2): for each output, gather its column. This is a one-time permute. For q_proj: (1152, 4096) → (4096, 1152). The bytes: 1152*4096 = 4.7MB, transpose is a permute (read 4.7MB, write 4.7MB). One-time. Fine. Similarly transpose scales (18, 4096) → (4096, 18) and zeros. Actually, this is a significant rewrite. Let me think about whether it's worth it vs just fixing occupancy via fusion + the int32 trick. Let me design it. **CUDA graph design:** The step has these data-dependent (per-step changing) quantities: - MLA cache length L (grows). - MLA position pos (= L before append). - The expert indices from topk (data-dependent but graph-safe — captured kernels recompute topk each replay). For the graph, all tensor SHAPES must be fixed. So: - c_kv buffer: fixed size (CAP, 512). Valid prefix [0, L). - k_rope buffer: fixed (CAP, 64). - L and pos: stored in device tensors (int32 scalars). Updated by captured kernels. The MLA attention ops that depend on L: - scores = q_abs @ c_kv.t() → if I use full c_kv (CAP rows), scores is (H, CAP). Fixed shape. But invalid rows [L, CAP) have garbage → must mask before softmax. - Masking: scores[:, L:] = -inf. L is a device scalar. I can compute a mask = arange(CAP) >= L (device tensor comparison) → (CAP,) bool, then scores = scores.masked_fill(mask[:,None], -inf). Fixed shape, uses device L. Graph-safe. - softmax over CAP with masked rows → masked rows get 0. - cv = p @ c_kv → (H, CAP) @ (CAP, 512) = (H, 512). Invalid p rows are 0. Fixed. ✓. - The cache append: buf[L] = c_kv_new. L is device scalar. `buf[L]` with tensor index → need scatter/index_put_. Graph-safe with fixed shapes. E.g., `buf.scatter(0, L_expanded, c_kv_new)` or a custom Triton kernel that reads L and writes. - pos for rope: cos/sin depend on pos. pos = L (before append). I compute rope from a pos tensor: angles = arange * inv * pos_tensor. Fixed shape. ✓. The KDA blocks (0,1,2) are fully L-independent — graph-trivial. Block 3 MoE is L-independent (after MLA). For a graph, the INPUTS (the tensors it reads) must be at fixed addresses, and I write new values into them before replay. Let me write the split-K kernel: ```python @triton.jit def _gemv_split_kernel(x_ptr, wq_ptr, s_ptr, z_ptr, idx_ptr, scratch_ptr, K, N, sxb, sxk, swe, swp, swn, sse, ssg, ssn, scb, scn, scs, # scratch strides (B, N, S) GROUP: tl.constexpr, HALF: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, NGPS: tl.constexpr): pid_b = tl.program_id(0) pid_n = tl.program_id(1) pid_s = tl.program_id(2) eid = tl.load(idx_ptr + pid_b).to(tl.int64) n_off = pid_n*BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) nm = n_off < N x_base = x_ptr + pid_b*sxb wq_base = wq_ptr + eid*swe s_base = s_ptr + eid*sse z_base = z_ptr + eid*sse acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_N], dtype=tl.float32) ha = tl.arange(0, HALF) g0 = pid_s * NGPS for gi in range(NGPS): g = g0 + gi ke = g*GROUP + 2*ha; ko = ke+1 xe = tl.load(x_base+ke*sxk).to(tl.float32) xo = tl.load(x_base+ko*sxk).to(tl.float32) pk = g*HALF + ha byte = tl.load(wq_base + pk[:,None]*swp + n_off[None,:]*swn, mask=nm[None,:], other=0) ib=byte.to(tl.int32); lo=(ib&0xF).to(tl.float32); hi=((ib>>4)&0xF).to(tl.float32) sc=tl.load(s_base+g*ssg+n_off*ssn, mask=nm, other=0).to(tl.float32) zo=tl.load(z_base+g*ssg+n_off*ssn, mask=nm, other=0).to(tl.float32) wl=(lo-zo[None,:])*sc[None,:]; wh=(hi-zo[None,:])*sc[None,:] acc += tl.sum(xe[:,None]*wl, axis=0) acc += tl.sum(xo[:,None]*wh, axis=0) # scratch[b, n, s] tl.store(scratch_ptr + pid_b*scb + n_off*scn + pid_s*scs, acc, mask=nm) @triton.jit def _reduce_kernel(scratch_ptr, y_ptr, N, S, scb, scn, scs, syb, syn, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr): pid_b = tl.program_id(0) pid_n = tl.program_id(1) n_off = pid_n*BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) nm = n_off < N s_arange = tl.arange(0, S) ptrs = scratch_ptr + pid_b*scb + n_off[:,None]*scn + s_arange[None,:]*scs vals = tl.load(ptrs, mask=nm[:,None], other=0) # (BLOCK_N, S) acc = tl.sum(vals, axis=1) # (BLOCK_N,) tl.store(y_ptr + pid_b*syb + n_off*syn, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=nm) ``` S must be constexpr (power of 2? for tl.arange). Let me step back and think hard about WHY this kernel is slow. Let me reconsider the arithmetic. For batch-1 GEMV, each weight byte contributes to one multiply-accumulate (after dequant). The dequant: per byte (2 int4), we do: unpack (bitwise, ~4 ops), 2 subtractions, 2 multiplies (scale), then 2 MACs (with x). So ~10 ops per 0.5 bytes = 20 ops/byte. At 463 GB/s = 463e9 bytes/s → 9.3e12 ops/s = 9.3 TFLOPS of scalar work. B200 CUDA cores: ~ how many? B200 has ~18,000 CUDA cores? At ~1.8 GHz, that's ~32 TFLOPS fp32 scalar. If the dequant math is the bottleneck (scalar integer/float ops per byte), then improving memory access won't help — I need to reduce the per-byte compute. 10 ops per byte of weight. The weight is int4 (4 bits). For W4A16, the "useful" compute is 1 MAC per int4 value = 1 MAC per 0.5 byte. But the dequant overhead (unpack, scale, zero) adds ~4-5 ops per value. So ~5-6 ops per 0.5 byte = 10-12 ops/byte. That overhead makes it compute-bound at low arithmetic intensity. This is a known issue with W4A16 on CUDA cores (no int4 tensor core support for this). The dequant overhead dominates. Solutions: 1. Reduce dequant ops: precompute (scale, -zero*scale) so dequant is one FMA: w = w_int * scale + bias where bias = -zero*scale. Then per value: w_int*scale + bias (1 FMA) instead of (w_int - zero)*scale (1 sub + 1 mul). Saves ~2 ops. And precompute per-group. Actually (w_int - z)*s = w_int*s - z*s. Precompute sz = z*s (per group, per output). Then w = w_int*s - sz. That's 1 mul + 1 sub = still 2 ops. Or FMA: w = fma(w_int, s, -sz) = 1 FMA. So 1 op instead of (sub+mul)=2. Halves dequant compute. Worth doing. But wait, I still need w_int (the unpacked nibble) which needs the bitwise unpack. The unpack: byte → lo (byte&0xF), hi ((byte>>4)&0xF). 2 ops for 2 values. Then for each: w = fma(w_int, s, neg_sz). So per byte: 2 (unpack) + 2 (FMA) = 4 ops + 2 MACs (×x) = 6 ops/byte = 3 ops/value. Better than 10. 2. Use tensor cores somehow? W4A16 doesn't map to bf16 tensor cores directly. Some kernels dequant to bf16 in registers and use tensor cores for the matmul — but for M=1 (GEMV), tensor cores are inefficient (need M≥8). So no. 3. Let me write the kernel carefully. ```python @triton.jit def _gemv_T_kernel(x_ptr, wq_ptr, s_ptr, z_ptr, idx_ptr, sc_ptr, K, N, NG, sxb, sxk, swe, sse, scb, scn, scs, GROUP: tl.constexpr, HALF: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, NGPS: tl.constexpr): pid_b = tl.program_id(0); pid_n = tl.program_id(1); pid_s = tl.program_id(2) eid = tl.load(idx_ptr + pid_b).to(tl.int64) n_off = pid_n*BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) nm = n_off < N xb = x_ptr + pid_b*sxb wqb = wq_ptr + eid*swe # base of (N, K//2) for this expert sb = s_ptr + eid*sse # (N, NG) zb = z_ptr + eid*sse acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_N], dtype=tl.float32) ha = tl.arange(0, HALF) g0 = pid_s*NGPS K2 = K//2 for gi in range(NGPS): g = g0 + gi; gm = g < NG ke = g*GROUP + 2*ha; ko = ke+1 xe = tl.load(xb + ke*sxk, mask=gm, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) xo = tl.load(xb + ko*sxk, mask=gm, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) pk = g*HALF + ha wptr = wqb + n_off[:,None]*K2 + pk[None,:] byte = tl.load(wptr, mask=nm[:,None] & gm, other=0) ib = byte.to(tl.int32) lo = (ib & 0xF).to(tl.float32); hi = ((ib>>4)&0xF).to(tl.float32) sc = tl.load(sb + n_off*NG + g, mask=nm & gm, other=0).to(tl.float32) zo = tl.load(zb + n_off*NG + g, mask=nm & gm, other=0).to(tl.float32) wl = (lo - zo[:,None])*sc[:,None] wh = (hi - zo[:,None])*sc[:,None] acc += tl.sum(xe[None,:]*wl, axis=1) acc += tl.sum(xo[None,:]*wh, axis=1) tl.store(sc_ptr + pid_b*scb + n_off*scn + pid_s*scs, acc, mask=nm) ``` Wait, K2 = K//2 — I should pass as a value or compute K//2. K is runtime, K2=K//2 runtime. Use in address: n_off[:,None]*K2. OK. The transposed (output-major) layout is a major win — nearly 2× the bandwidth (qkvg: 18.5µs @ 1085 GB/s vs 40µs; and it actually beats torch's bf16 matmul in equivalent terms since int4 is 4× denser). The contiguous (BLOCK_N, K//2) streaming is the key. Let me integrate this: transpose weights to output-major at preprocess time, with split-K + reduce for the small B1 cases.

 GEMV so the int4 weights are streamed once (never materialized to bf16), and by
 absorbing the MLA kv_b projection into the query so the (L, 8192) latent
 expansion is never materialized at long context.
+
+Key implementation choices:
+  * int4 weights are stored OUTPUT-MAJOR (N, K//2) so each program streams a
+    fully contiguous (BLOCK_N, K//2) weight tile (roughly 2x the bandwidth of
+    the input-major layout for a batch-1 GEMV).
+  * projections sharing an input are fused (q/k/v/g -> one GEMV; MoE gate+up).
+  * split-K (with a tiny reduce) is used to lift occupancy for the small-N,
+    single-input linears.
+  * MLA keeps scores in (H, L) so the softmax reduces over the contiguous dim.

assistant

Confirmed: host dispatch is 2.78ms/step but GPU work is only 1.28ms/step — the CPU is the bottleneck (~110 kernels × ~25µs dispatch). The transposed kernel did cut GPU gemv time (0.85ms→0.32ms). The fix is CUDA graphs, which collapses host dispatch to ~0. This requires: persistent fixed-shape state buffers, in-place state updates, and MLA cache masking (full CAP buffer + device-side length counter). Let me record this finding and build it.

 """Fused W4A16 dequant-GEMV solution for the Kimi-Linear hybrid decode unit.
 
 Beats baseline.py by fusing the int4 unpack + per-group dequant directly into the
-GEMV so the int4 weights are streamed once (never materialized to bf16), and by
-absorbing the MLA kv_b projection into the query so the (L, 8192) latent
-expansion is never materialized at long context.
-
-Key implementation choices:
-  * int4 weights are stored OUTPUT-MAJOR (N, K//2) so each program streams a

-    the input-major layout for a batch-1 GEMV).

NO. softplus(x)=log(1+e^x). -softplus(gp). ``` Hmm, ge = exp(-softplus(gp)). softplus in Triton: `tl.log(1 + tl.exp(gp))`. So ge = exp(-log(1+exp(gp))) = 1/(1+exp(gp)). Oh nice! ge = 1/(1+exp(gp)) = sigmoid(-gp). So ge = sigmoid(-gp). That's cheap (1 sigmoid). Wait: g = -softplus(gp) = -log(1+e^gp). ge = g.exp() = e^{-log(1+e^gp)} = 1/(1+e^gp) = sigmoid(-gp). Yes! So: ``` ge = tl.sigmoid(-gp) # (DK,) beta = tl.sigmoid(tl.load(bp_ptr + pid_h).to(tl.float32)) # scalar v_tile = tl.load(v_ptr + pid_h*sv0 + d_off, mask=dm).to(tl.float32) # (BLOCK_D,) S_base = S_ptr + pid_h*sS0 # S_tile = S[:, d_off] shape (DK, BLOCK_D): rows j, cols d S_ptrs = S_base + j[:,None]*sS1 + d_off[None,:]*sS2 S_tile = tl.load(S_ptrs, mask=dm[None,:], other=0.0) # (DK, BLOCK_D) fp32 S_tile = S_tile * ge[:,None] pred = tl.sum(S_tile * k[:,None], axis=0) # (BLOCK_D,) S_tile = S_tile + beta * k[:,None] * (v_tile - pred)[None,:] o_tile = tl.sum(S_tile * q[:,None], axis=0) # (BLOCK_D,) tl.store(S_ptrs, S_tile, mask=dm[None,:]) tl.store(o_ptr + pid_h*DK + d_off, o_tile, mask=dm) ``` Wait, S strides: S is (H, Dk, Dk) contiguous → sS0=Dk*Dk=16384, sS1=Dk=128, sS2=1. Let me write the kernel: ```python @triton.jit def _kda_kernel(S_ptr, o_ptr, q_ptr, k_ptr, v_ptr, gp_ptr, bp_ptr, Dk, scale, sS0, sS1, sS2, DK: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_D: tl.constexpr): pid_h = tl.program_id(0) pid_d = tl.program_id(1) d_off = pid_d*BLOCK_D + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_D) dm = d_off < DK j = tl.arange(0, DK) q = tl.load(q_ptr + pid_h*Dk + j).to(tl.float32) * scale k = tl.load(k_ptr + pid_h*Dk + j).to(tl.float32) gp = tl.load(gp_ptr + pid_h*Dk + j).to(tl.float32) ge = tl.sigmoid(-gp) beta = tl.sigmoid(tl.load(bp_ptr + pid_h).to(tl.float32)) v = tl.load(v_ptr + pid_h*Dk + d_off, mask=dm, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) Sb = S_ptr + pid_h*sS0 Sptrs = Sb + j[:,None]*sS1 + d_off[None,:]*sS2 S = tl.load(Sptrs, mask=dm[None,:], other=0.0) S = S * ge[:,None] pred = tl.sum(S * k[:,None], axis=0) S = S + beta * k[:,None] * (v - pred)[None,:] o = tl.sum(S * q[:,None], axis=0) tl.store(Sptrs, S, mask=dm[None,:]) tl.store(o_ptr + pid_h*Dk + d_off, o.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=dm) ``` DK=Dk=128 (constexpr). assistant

Now GPU-bound at 1.28ms. Breakdown: copy_/conversions ~0.65ms (50%), gemv 0.27ms, rest small. The eager dtype conversions and KDA state elementwise dominate. Two changes: (1) store MLA _Wk/_Wv as fp32 once (kills 2 big per-step copies), (2) fuse the entire KDA recurrent-state update (S*ge, pred, rank-1 update, o) into one Triton kernel — eliminating ~10 ops on the 2MB S tensor per KDA block.

Note: ge = exp(-softplus(gp)) = sigmoid(-gp) — a clean identity that simplifies the kernel.
